<div id="review_1283646728" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1283646728" />
    <a title="Ian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3812181-ian"><img alt="Ian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1412859542p2/3812181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1283646728?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian" href="/user/show/3812181-ian">Ian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3812181-ian?shelf=favourite-books">favourite-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1283646728" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10482009388230663382">I'd never read Sartre before and was expecting a difficult read. Whilst I probably didn't pick up on all the philosophical themes I did thoroughly enjoy this tale of fairly unlikeable people and their relationships with each other. There's little fun to be had with this novel but it does convey a remarkable depth of characterisation, nobody comes out of it very well and indeed none of the cast seem particularly enamored with each other. <br/><br/>It all takes place over a very brief period of time just be</span>
  <span id="freeText10482009388230663382" style="display:none">I'd never read Sartre before and was expecting a difficult read. Whilst I probably didn't pick up on all the philosophical themes I did thoroughly enjoy this tale of fairly unlikeable people and their relationships with each other. There's little fun to be had with this novel but it does convey a remarkable depth of characterisation, nobody comes out of it very well and indeed none of the cast seem particularly enamored with each other. <br /><br />It all takes place over a very brief period of time just before the second world war. There's a terrible sense of foreboding, the book drips bleakness. The main character, Mathieu, has gotten his girlfriend, Marcelle, pregnant and wants her to abort, he craves freedom and desperately doesn't want to fit into society. He makes presumptions of her and thinks he is in love with someone else. Who in turn isn't in love with him and is more concerned with her exams. Mathieu's best friend is obsessed with suicide but doesn't have the conviction to see it through, he is appalled at the way Mathieu is treating Marcelle and connives against him. As the book progresses Marcelle is prepared to go to any lengths to raise the money for an abortion not realising that his quest is taking him further down a path of self destruction... although ultimately the freedom he desires. <br /><br />It's that freedom which resonates throughout the book, what does it mean to be free, how can it be attained whilst simultaneously trying to exist in a life which has no meaning. It's a very thought provoking book, I can't pretend to have grasped all the existentialist stuff but followed the general gist of it... and the characters and their duplicitous machinations were fascinating enough to keep me turning pages. I shall be reading more Sartre.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10482009388230663382" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_12472138" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12472138" />
    <a title="Cody" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/761426-cody"><img alt="Cody" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1282525942p2/761426.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12472138?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cody" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cody" href="/user/show/761426-cody">Cody</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12472138" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2023716939234804679">I read Nausea by Sartre while in college and really go into Existentialism and novels based on Existential themes. After Nausea (which is great!) I had only read a few essays and some short stories by Sartre. Now, a few years later, I wanted to get back into Sartre and I thought I would start by reading his Freedom trilogy. I began with The Age of reason, a story about a man dealing with the inevitability of becoming middle-aged and possibly becoming a father. The catch is...he is neither ready </span>
  <span id="freeText2023716939234804679" style="display:none">I read Nausea by Sartre while in college and really go into Existentialism and novels based on Existential themes. After Nausea (which is great!) I had only read a few essays and some short stories by Sartre. Now, a few years later, I wanted to get back into Sartre and I thought I would start by reading his Freedom trilogy. I began with The Age of reason, a story about a man dealing with the inevitability of becoming middle-aged and possibly becoming a father. The catch is...he is neither ready to grow up nor become a father.<br /><br />The theme of Freedom is completely inherent throughout the novel as the Mathieu realizes that growing older and being a father forces you to have responsibility and basically lays the rest of your life out for you. Mathieu is not ready for commitment of any kind and you read as his life spirals somewhat out of control as he deals with all of these altering issues.<br /><br />Another theme of freedom runs through the veins of the novel, but instead of it being someone’s immediate life, it is subtlety presented as political freedom. Not only is the story happening on the eve of WWII, but Mathieu is also being tugged back and forth between several political parties.<br /><br />This novel is rich, deep and full of little motifs that add up to the large personal struggle of one man and the rest of his life. Maybe not the best book for summer (I read it in the winter) but one that will make you fully think about the long term effect each choice you make will impact the rest of your life.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2023716939234804679" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/139717183?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2011">2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer139717183" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4584512534187920617">There's a bit where Sartre describes Mathieu's sister-in-law: she's pretty, but "Mathieu had on countless occasions tried to unify these fluid features, but they escaped him; as a face, Odette's always seemed to be dissolving, and thus retained its elusive bourgeois mystery." (p. 127) And that's a little how I feel about this book, halfway through; it's certainly very good, and pretty to look at, but it's weirdly slippery. I can't quite get a handle on it.<br/><br/>That may be my fault. Tough to say what </span>
  <span id="freeText4584512534187920617" style="display:none">There's a bit where Sartre describes Mathieu's sister-in-law: she's pretty, but "Mathieu had on countless occasions tried to unify these fluid features, but they escaped him; as a face, Odette's always seemed to be dissolving, and thus retained its elusive bourgeois mystery." (p. 127) And that's a little how I feel about this book, halfway through; it's certainly very good, and pretty to look at, but it's weirdly slippery. I can't quite get a handle on it.<br /><br />That may be my fault. Tough to say what you bring to a book, and what the book brings to you.<br /><br />And there's a sense of foreboding hanging over the thing that makes me feel like this won't always be the story; like something might happen soon to throw everyone into sharper relief. Maybe the whole thing is like the Gauguin self-portrait that Mathieu takes Ivich to see: so far we're just drawing the shadowy figures behind him, and soon enough we'll show the figure in front.<br /><br />Sorry, I got a little flowery there. <br /><br />---------------------------------------------------<br /><br />And it ended like and unlike how I expected. I don't want to say too much, 'cause, y'know, spoilers and all that. It was beautiful, and I feel like getting screamingly plastered now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4584512534187920617" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11518500149205788817">One of my favorites. However, I only appreciated it once I reached a certain level of maturity. It dives into the deeper consciousness of each character underneath the basic thoughts. It makes you realize how insecure and all over the place all of humanity really is. The message I get from this book is "Miserable? You are not alone."</span>

<div id="review_3440169463" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3440169463" />
    <a title="Ida" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6138133-ida"><img alt="Ida" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456952061p2/6138133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3440169463?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ida" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ida" href="/user/show/6138133-ida">Ida</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780241259696" href="/book/show/30118093-the-age-of-reason">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3440169463" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17894795462449990565">The first book on Roads to Freedom (Le Chemins de la Liberte). It centers on the main character Mathieu Delarue, a 34 year old college professor, with a rather left leaning political view and feel that he lives his life to the fullest. He was in a relationship, but prefers to call it "having a mistress", for 7 years. Both he and his mistress were agree not to formalize their relationship and did not want to have children. Those kind of commitment would just became a burden and limited Mathieu's </span>
  <span id="freeText17894795462449990565" style="display:none">The first book on Roads to Freedom (Le Chemins de la Liberte). It centers on the main character Mathieu Delarue, a 34 year old college professor, with a rather left leaning political view and feel that he lives his life to the fullest. He was in a relationship, but prefers to call it "having a mistress", for 7 years. Both he and his mistress were agree not to formalize their relationship and did not want to have children. Those kind of commitment would just became a burden and limited Mathieu's freedom. Until one day, his mistress Marcelle got pregnant and without having any further conversation decided to abort the baby. He jumped into conclusion that Marcelle did not want to have a baby. <br /><br />Mathieu wanted the abortion done by a reputable doctor, a well-known Jewish doctor who left his hometown in Austria to avoid concentration camp asked for 4 thousands franc. He did not have that much money, so he asked for a soft loan from his acquaintances. Among others is his brother Jacques, a rich successful solicitor. Jacques against Mathieu's idea to abort the baby, but he would loan Mathieu his money only if Mathieu used it to marry Marcelle. Jacques also criticised Mathieu's way of living. He said to Mathieu that Marcelle's pregnancy is the logical result of Mathieu's free will and that he wanted to suppress it because he would not accept all the consequences of his acts. <br /><br />"I dare say you aren't lying to yourself at this precise moment: the trouble is that your whole life is built upon a life" Jacques continued with, "You are trying...to evade the fact that you're bourgeois and ashamed of it. I myself reverted to bourgeois after my aberrations, and contracted a marriage of convenience with the party, but you are a bourgeois by taste and temperament, and it's your temperament that's pushing you into marriage. For you are married, Mathieu"<br /><br />Other than disapproved with the idea of marriage, Mathieu also refused to be part of the Communist party, despite the fact that he frequently criticised the government's policy, especially now that Europe was on the brink of World War II. He said it to his friend Brunet, "Well, there it is. I can't join, I haven't enough reasons for doing so. I am as angry as you are, and with the same people and the same things, but not violently enough. I can't help it. If I started marching past, lifting my fist and singing the International, and if I proclaimed myself satisfied with all that, I should be telling myself a lie."<br /><br />The book continues with Mathieu's effort to get the four-thousand franc loan from his acquaintances, and his relationship with other human beings with their own flawless, namely Daniel a rather narcissist 'friend', brother and sister Boris and Ivich, with whom Mathieu also have a special feeling, Lola who was in love with Boris.<br /><br />I am not an expert in existentialism. For my perspective, The Age of Reason is a story about humans life with their strengths and flaws, their desire to life, their freedom in choosing the path that life has offered. In other words, humans are doomed to choose. Even in refusing to make a choice, according to Sartre, we still, nonetheless, make the choice not to choose, and so remain responsible for the course that our lives take. Many people, like Mathieu, would like to deny this fact, but at a deep level, we all recognize it. Though we would like to relieve ourselves of responsibility for how our lives turn out, in fact we have no one to blame except ourselves. We are the only ones responsible for who we have become. <br /><br />Denial of the fact that humans are doomed to choose will eventually reached its peak, when a human reach a certain age. It is the age of reason. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17894795462449990565" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_540402528" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/540402528" />
    <a title="Don" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1478469-don"><img alt="Don" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420179358p2/1478469.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/540402528?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Don" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Don" href="/user/show/1478469-don">Don</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141185286" href="/book/show/19371560-the-age-of-reason">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1478469-don?shelf=european-novel">european-novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer540402528" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14104704881311917613">The BBC’s famous production of ‘Roads to Freedom’ , broadcast in 1970 was a seminal experience for a whole generation of Brits who were marked by the events of May 1968 and the prospect of a late 20th century dominated by the intellectual insights of the Mediterranean and Latin world, and the displacement of hegemony of the Anglo-Saxon.<br/><br/>I was one of them. In those days it felt like the world was in revolt and the only decent thing to do was join the revolution. The French had thrown their bourgeo</span>
  <span id="freeText14104704881311917613" style="display:none">The BBC’s famous production of ‘Roads to Freedom’ , broadcast in 1970 was a seminal experience for a whole generation of Brits who were marked by the events of May 1968 and the prospect of a late 20th century dominated by the intellectual insights of the Mediterranean and Latin world, and the displacement of hegemony of the Anglo-Saxon.<br /><br />I was one of them. In those days it felt like the world was in revolt and the only decent thing to do was join the revolution. The French had thrown their bourgeois republic into turmoil with mass actions that had brought the working class into alliance with youthful intellectuals and the urgent need seemed to be how to understand the thoughts and outlooks of the people who were populating this new world.<br /><br />Something of this was set out in Sartre’s tale of Mathieu Delarue, the Parisian philosophy professor who lived through the dilemmas of his personal life. An eight year long relationship with the reclusive Marcelle has been going nowhere for a long time. An infatuation with Ivich, the damaged and emotionally constrained sister of Boris, his student, offers no prospect of satisfaction. The two other significant figures in his life, long-time friends Brunet and Daniel, and the mirror opposites of the way to live life -one a committed communist, the other an introvert and self-reproachful homosexual – throw out other challenges as to the proper way to lead life, but not ones available to Mathieu.<br /><br />Matters come to a head in the opening pages when Marcelle tells Mathieu that she is pregnant. It appears they are agreed that an abortion is needed and Mathieu sets off to procure the money which is needed. He first of all contemplates his own wretched financial position, causing despondency about the rut he is caught in, and the fact that he will be required to approach others, his brother, Daniel, for a loan. This is refused, but with the opportunity being given for lectures condemning him as someone who has failed to accept responsibility for the state of his life.<br /><br />The action develops by way journeys through the streets of Paris, by foot and by taxi, the contemplation of acts of cruelty (the drowning of cats), petty law breaking (the theft of a book), and the misery of an older woman, Lola, entangled in an affair with the feckless Boris. The memorable nightclub scene, in which most of the main characters are brought together to mud wrestle in their mutual misery, produces the electrifying moment of acts of self-mutilation as the only way in which people can act as free beings in this life.<br />Mathieu comes to the realisation that Marcelle might wish to keep the child and that his fate is therefore marriage, but this engages none of his emotions except despair. His belief that he should aspire to freedom, even if that state held out no hope of happiness, drives him towards what proves to be an act which is destructive of what remains of his relationship with Marcelle. He is left alone, without the consolation of any philosophy that would explain his actions and provide solace.<br /> <br />There is a bigger backdrop to what might seem the mundane, banal events of an incompetently led life. Across the borders the Nazi regime threatens war. In another direction the Spanish republic fights for its life. Mathieu wonders whether an engagement with these bigger themes might offer him a way forward, but diffidence holds him back from taking the decisions which might make him an effective actor in the face of these developments. The struggle for freedom is disconnected from politics and looks more like a personal attitude to life, as the individual moves listlessly and hopelessly to recreate the infantile perception of a world which is ordered to provide for his emotional and material needs.<br /><br />We made a mistake thinking that the pragmatic verities of the Anglo-Saxon world had been eclipsed by the modernism of the Latins. Sartre, the Mathieu of his own life, finally committed himself to revolutionary socialism, but more by reason of his desire for freedom in the sense of scope for personal action rather than any convictions about Marxism. But at least we got a truly great series of novels from the ride. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14104704881311917613" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_335146335" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/335146335" />
    <a title="Perry Whitford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5485442-perry-whitford"><img alt="Perry Whitford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1306266009p2/5485442.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/335146335?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Perry Whitford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Perry Whitford" href="/user/show/5485442-perry-whitford">Perry Whitford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140181777" href="/book/show/1334266.The_Age_of_Reason">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer335146335" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1283692525368858269">The first book of Satre's <i>Roads of Freedom</i> trilogy, set in the Paris of 1938, with war and occupation still an unlikely prospect. <br/><br/>The main character, school teacher Mathieu, flirts with bohemianism and communism, while rather ignobly trying to force his mistress to have an abortion, asking friends and family to help him out so that he can retain his freedom.<br/><br/>Not very nice behaviour, but what the hell, existence precedes essence right? Therefore all behaviour is essentially meaningless. Handy excu</span>
  <span id="freeText1283692525368858269" style="display:none">The first book of Satre's <i>Roads of Freedom</i> trilogy, set in the Paris of 1938, with war and occupation still an unlikely prospect. <br /><br />The main character, school teacher Mathieu, flirts with bohemianism and communism, while rather ignobly trying to force his mistress to have an abortion, asking friends and family to help him out so that he can retain his freedom.<br /><br />Not very nice behaviour, but what the hell, existence precedes essence right? Therefore all behaviour is essentially meaningless. Handy excuse, that.<br /><br />It's a long time since I read this book, over twenty years in fact, but I still remember being strongly impressed by it as a teenager. I had not till that point read anything with such depth of consciousness across multiple characters, the warts and all frankness of their thoughts and motives for action. <br /><br />Up until that point I had not been introduced to the idea of a protagonist who could be so flawed and dislikable. It was something of a shock to me, albeit an instructive one. <br /><br />I read the third book of the trilogy too, but I can't recall reading the middle book, <i>The Reprieve</i>, although I may have. I will try and read that one soon, which will enable me to better revisit my fading impressions of this novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1283692525368858269" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3139060445" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12867371925180258851">I was in second year high school when I read this ghastly thing. Sartre was at the summit of his glory as a pop culture figure. Having read Camus, Jules Verne, George Orwell and others in Penguin traditions I had great faith in anything that they published.<br/><br/>In retrospect I see that I had put my faith in all the wrong people. "The Age of Reason" is a miserable and insignficant novel about being Jean Paul Sartre. At the time, I was too young to understand that I was being had. I went on to read the</span>
  <span id="freeText12867371925180258851" style="display:none">I was in second year high school when I read this ghastly thing. Sartre was at the summit of his glory as a pop culture figure. Having read Camus, Jules Verne, George Orwell and others in Penguin traditions I had great faith in anything that they published.<br /><br />In retrospect I see that I had put my faith in all the wrong people. "The Age of Reason" is a miserable and insignficant novel about being Jean Paul Sartre. At the time, I was too young to understand that I was being had. I went on to read the next two novels in the wretched "Chemins de la Liberte" trilogy which adds nothing to the glory of Sartre who wrote many things that are much better.</span>

<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_15682'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15682.A_Happy_Death"><img alt="A Happy Death" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1348556147l/15682._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('duvjvdilpw0/hQ3WxbJVnYJhkYToAgRLGs0DxxhqJhRMzYpvY/tuZZiuubcyR19CVD52TCaPiixBs0V21SnUjg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fRvrNY4/SeknQshKu1Y+4oFNksZ31H7dlVWrqCfBy25HPYLnNWGAgYBpfCtMozQ9VxJ1DrlZ8LrOK+0Z6oI59A==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10154072959118539359"><b>Jean-Paul Charles Aymard Sartre</b>, normally known simply as Jean-Paul Sartre, was a French existentialist philosopher and pioneer, dramatist and screenwriter, novelist and critic. He was a leading figure in 20th century French philosophy.<br/><br/>He declined the award of the 1964 Nobel Prize in Literature "for his work which, rich in ideas and filled with the spirit of freedom and the quest for truth, has ex</span>
  <span id="freeText10154072959118539359" style="display:none"><b>Jean-Paul Charles Aymard Sartre</b>, normally known simply as Jean-Paul Sartre, was a French existentialist philosopher and pioneer, dramatist and screenwriter, novelist and critic. He was a leading figure in 20th century French philosophy.<br /><br />He declined the award of the 1964 Nobel Prize in Literature "for his work which, rich in ideas and filled with the spirit of freedom and the quest for truth, has exerted a far-reaching influence on our age."<br /><br />In the years around the time of his death, however, existentialism declined in French philosophy and was overtaken by structuralism, represented by Levi-Strauss and, one of Sartre's detractors, Michel Foucault.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10154072959118539359" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
